<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_(cropped).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="509" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png/250px-Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png/375px-Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png/500px-Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_%28cropped%29.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biofluorescence_in_Ceratophrys_cranwelli_-_41598_2020_59528_Fig2-bottom_(cropped).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many amphibians—like this <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceratophrys_cranwelli" title="Ceratophrys cranwelli">Ceratophrys cranwelli</a></i>—exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofluorescence" title="Biofluorescence">biofluorescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LambDavis2020_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-LambDavis2020-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Amphibians</b> are <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapod</a> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> <b>Amphibia</b>. All living amphibians belong to the group <a href="/wiki/Lissamphibia" title="Lissamphibia">Lissamphibia</a>. They inhabit a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>, with most species living within <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fossorial" title="Fossorial">fossorial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">arboreal</a> or freshwater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystems" title="Aquatic ecosystems">aquatic ecosystems</a>. Thus amphibians typically start out as <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> living in water, but some species have developed behavioural adaptations to bypass this.
</p><p>The young generally undergo <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> from larva with gills to an adult air-breathing form with <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>. Amphibians use their skin as a secondary respiratory surface and some small terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a> and frogs lack lungs and rely entirely on their skin. They are superficially similar to <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> like <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> but, along with mammals and birds, reptiles are <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> and do not require water bodies in which to breed. With their complex reproductive needs and permeable skins, amphibians are often <a href="/wiki/Ecological_indicator" title="Ecological indicator">ecological indicators</a>; in recent decades there has been a dramatic <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">decline in amphibian populations</a> for many species around the globe.
</p><p>The earliest amphibians <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_tetrapods" title="Evolution of tetrapods">evolved</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarcopterygian" title="Sarcopterygian">sarcopterygian</a> fish with lungs and bony-limbed fins, features that were helpful in adapting to dry land. They diversified and became dominant during the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> periods, but were later displaced by reptiles and other vertebrates. The origin of modern amphibians belonging to Lissamphibia, which first appeared during the Early Triassic, around 250 million years ago, has long been contentious. However the emerging consensus is that they likely originated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temnospondyls" title="Temnospondyls">temnospondyls</a>, the most diverse group of prehistoric amphibians, during the Permian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The three modern orders of amphibians are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anura_(frog)" title="Anura (frog)">Anura</a> (the frogs), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urodela" title="Urodela">Urodela</a> (the salamanders), and <a href="/wiki/Caecilian" title="Caecilian">Apoda</a> (the caecilians), a fourth group, the <a href="/wiki/Albanerpetontidae" title="Albanerpetontidae">Albanerpetontidae</a>, became extinct around 2 million years ago. The number of known amphibian species is approximately 8,000, of which nearly 90% are frogs. The smallest amphibian (and vertebrate) in the world is a frog from <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis" title="Paedophryne amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></i>) with a length of just 7.7 mm (0.30 in). The largest living amphibian is the 1.8 m (5 ft 11 in) <a href="/wiki/South_China_giant_salamander" title="South China giant salamander">South China giant salamander</a> (<i>Andrias sligoi</i>), but this is dwarfed by prehistoric temnospondyls such as <i><a href="/wiki/Mastodonsaurus" title="Mastodonsaurus">Mastodonsaurus</a></i> which could reach up to 6 metres in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The study of amphibians is called <a href="/wiki/Batrachology" title="Batrachology">batrachology</a>, while the study of both reptiles and amphibians is called <a href="/wiki/Herpetology" title="Herpetology">herpetology</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_amphibians" title="List of amphibians">List of amphibians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_(LSUMZ_95004).png"><img alt="World's smallest vertebrate" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png/220px-Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png/330px-Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png/440px-Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_%28LSUMZ_95004%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paratype_of_Paedophryne_amauensis_(LSUMZ_95004).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The world's smallest known vertebrate, <i><a href="/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis" title="Paedophryne amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></i>, sitting on a <a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">U.S. dime</a>. The dime is 17.9 mm in diameter, for scale</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>amphibian</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> term <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀμφίβιος</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">amphíbios</i></span>), which means 'both kinds of life', <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀμφί</span></span> meaning 'of both kinds' and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">βιος</span></span> meaning 'life'. The term was initially used as a general adjective for animals that could live on land or in water, including seals and otters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Traditionally, the class Amphibia includes all tetrapod vertebrates that are not amniotes. Amphibia in its widest sense (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_lato" title="Sensu lato">sensu lato</a></i></span>) was divided into three <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">subclasses</a>, two of which are extinct:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Lepospondyli" title="Lepospondyli">Lepospondyli</a>† (small Paleozoic group, which are more closely related to amniotes than Lissamphibia)</li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Temnospondyli" title="Temnospondyli">Temnospondyli</a>† (diverse Paleozoic and early Mesozoic grade)</li>
<li>Subclass <a href="/wiki/Lissamphibia" title="Lissamphibia">Lissamphibia</a> (all modern amphibians, including frogs, toads, salamanders, newts and caecilians)
<ul><li>Salientia (frogs, <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a> and relatives): Jurassic to present—7,360 current species in 53 families<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calif_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calif-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>Caudata (<a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newt" title="Newt">newts</a> and relatives): Jurassic to present—764 current species in 9 families<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calif_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calif-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gymnophiona (<a href="/wiki/Caecilian" title="Caecilian">caecilians</a> and relatives): Jurassic to present—215 current species in 10 families<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calif_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calif-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>Allocaudata† (<a href="/wiki/Albanerpetontidae" title="Albanerpetontidae">Albanerpetontidae</a>) Middle Jurassic – Early Pleistocene</li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triadobatrachus.jpg"><img alt="Triadobatrachus massinoti" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="842" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Triadobatrachus.jpg/220px-Triadobatrachus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Triadobatrachus.jpg/330px-Triadobatrachus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Triadobatrachus.jpg/440px-Triadobatrachus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triadobatrachus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Triadobatrachus" title="Triadobatrachus">Triadobatrachus massinoti</a></i>, a proto-frog from the Early Triassic of Madagascar</div></div></div>
<p>The actual number of species in each group depends on the taxonomic classification followed. The two most common systems are the classification adopted by the website AmphibiaWeb, <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, and the classification by <a href="/wiki/Herpetology" title="Herpetology">herpetologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Darrel_Frost" title="Darrel Frost">Darrel Frost</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, available as the online reference database "Amphibian Species of the World".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The numbers of species cited above follows Frost and the total number of known amphibian species as of March 31, 2019, is exactly 8,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> of which nearly 90% are frogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-species_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-species-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> classification, the taxon <a href="/wiki/Labyrinthodontia" title="Labyrinthodontia">Labyrinthodontia</a> has been discarded as it is a <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">polyparaphyletic</a> group without unique defining features apart from <a href="/wiki/Cladistics#plesiomorphy" title="Cladistics">shared primitive characteristics</a>. Classification varies according to the preferred phylogeny of the author and whether they use a <a href="/wiki/Cladistics#Three_definitions_of_clade" title="Cladistics">stem-based or a node-based</a> classification. Traditionally, amphibians as a class are defined as all tetrapods with a larval stage, while the group that includes the common ancestors of all living amphibians (frogs, salamanders and caecilians) and all their descendants is called Lissamphibia. The phylogeny of Paleozoic amphibians is uncertain, and Lissamphibia may possibly fall within extinct groups, like the Temnospondyli (traditionally placed in the subclass Labyrinthodontia) or the Lepospondyli, and in some analyses even in the amniotes. This means that advocates of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">phylogenetic nomenclature</a> have removed a large number of <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> Devonian and Carboniferous amphibian-type tetrapod groups that were formerly placed in Amphibia in <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean taxonomy</a>, and included them elsewhere under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cladistic_taxonomy" title="Cladistic taxonomy">cladistic taxonomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackburnWake_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlackburnWake-2">[2]</a></sup> If the common ancestor of amphibians and amniotes is included in Amphibia, it becomes a paraphyletic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>All modern amphibians are included in the subclass Lissamphibia, which is usually considered a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, a group of species that have evolved from a common ancestor. The three modern orders are Anura (the frogs), Caudata (or Urodela, the salamanders), and Gymnophiona (or Apoda, the caecilians).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953-13">[13]</a></sup> It has been suggested that salamanders arose separately from a Temnospondyl-like ancestor, and even that caecilians are the sister group of the advanced <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptiliomorph</a> amphibians, and thus of amniotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-14">[14]</a></sup> Although the fossils of several older proto-frogs with primitive characteristics are known, the oldest "true frog" is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosalirus_bitis" title="Prosalirus bitis">Prosalirus bitis</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Jurassic" title="Early Jurassic">Early Jurassic</a> <a href="/wiki/Kayenta_Formation" title="Kayenta Formation">Kayenta Formation</a> of Arizona. It is anatomically very similar to modern frogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The oldest known caecilian is another Early Jurassic species, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eocaecilia_micropodia" title="Eocaecilia micropodia">Eocaecilia micropodia</a></i>, also from Arizona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The earliest salamander is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beiyanerpeton_jianpingensis" title="Beiyanerpeton jianpingensis">Beiyanerpeton jianpingensis</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Jurassic" title="Late Jurassic">Late Jurassic</a> of northeastern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Authorities disagree as to whether Salientia is a superorder that includes the order Anura, or whether Anura is a sub-order of the order Salientia. The Lissamphibia are traditionally divided into three <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">orders</a>, but an extinct salamander-like family, the Albanerpetontidae, is now considered part of Lissamphibia alongside the superorder Salientia. Furthermore, Salientia includes all three recent orders plus the Triassic proto-frog, <i><a href="/wiki/Triadobatrachus" title="Triadobatrachus">Triadobatrachus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_tetrapods" title="Evolution of tetrapods">Evolution of tetrapods</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_prehistoric_amphibians" title="List of prehistoric amphibians">List of prehistoric amphibians</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg/220px-Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg/330px-Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg/440px-Eusthenopteron_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiktaalik_BW.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tiktaalik_BW.jpg/220px-Tiktaalik_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tiktaalik_BW.jpg/330px-Tiktaalik_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tiktaalik_BW.jpg/440px-Tiktaalik_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Top:</b> Restoration of <i><a href="/wiki/Eusthenopteron" title="Eusthenopteron">Eusthenopteron</a></i>, a fully aquatic lobe-finned fish
<b>Bottom:</b> Restoration of <i><a href="/wiki/Tiktaalik" title="Tiktaalik">Tiktaalik</a></i>, an advanced tetrapodomorph fish</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The first major groups of amphibians developed in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period, around 370 million years ago, from <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fish</a> which were similar to the modern <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evoamphib_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-evoamphib-19">[19]</a></sup> These ancient lobe-finned fish had evolved multi-jointed leg-like fins with digits that enabled them to crawl along the sea bottom. Some fish had developed primitive lungs that help them breathe air when the stagnant pools of the Devonian swamps were low in oxygen. They could also use their strong fins to hoist themselves out of the water and onto dry land if circumstances so required. Eventually, their bony fins would <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolve</a> into limbs and they would become the ancestors to all <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, including modern amphibians, reptiles, birds, and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. Despite being able to crawl on land, many of these prehistoric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapodomorph" title="Tetrapodomorph">tetrapodomorph</a> fish still spent most of their time in the water. They had started to develop lungs, but still breathed predominantly with gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many examples of species showing <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">transitional features</a> have been discovered. <i><a href="/wiki/Ichthyostega" title="Ichthyostega">Ichthyostega</a></i> was one of the first primitive amphibians, with nostrils and more efficient lungs. It had four sturdy limbs, a neck, a tail with fins and a skull very similar to that of the lobe-finned fish, <i><a href="/wiki/Eusthenopteron" title="Eusthenopteron">Eusthenopteron</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evoamphib_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-evoamphib-19">[19]</a></sup> Amphibians evolved adaptations that allowed them to stay out of the water for longer periods. Their lungs improved and their skeletons became heavier and stronger, better able to support the weight of their bodies on land. They developed "hands" and "feet" with five or more digits;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ichthyostega_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ichthyostega-21">[21]</a></sup> the skin became more capable of retaining body fluids and resisting desiccation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-20">[20]</a></sup> The fish's <a href="/wiki/Hyomandibula" title="Hyomandibula">hyomandibula</a> bone in the <a href="/wiki/Hyoid_bone" title="Hyoid bone">hyoid</a> region behind the gills diminished in size and became the <a href="/wiki/Stapes" title="Stapes">stapes</a> of the amphibian ear, an adaptation necessary for hearing on dry land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> An affinity between the amphibians and the <a href="/wiki/Teleost" title="Teleost">teleost</a> fish is the multi-folded structure of the teeth and the paired <a href="/wiki/Occipital_bone" title="Occipital bone">supra-occipital bones</a> at the back of the head, neither of these features being found elsewhere in the animal kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoczynska_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spoczynska-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg"><img alt="Diplocaulus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1428" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg/170px-Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg/255px-Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg/340px-Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diplocaulus_vale21DB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Permian lepospondyl <i><a href="/wiki/Diplocaulus" title="Diplocaulus">Diplocaulus</a></i> was largely aquatic</div></div></div>
<p>At the end of the Devonian period (360 million years ago), the seas, rivers and lakes were teeming with life while the land was the realm of early plants and devoid of vertebrates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoczynska_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spoczynska-23">[23]</a></sup> though some, such as <i>Ichthyostega</i>, may have sometimes hauled themselves out of the water. It is thought they may have propelled themselves with their forelimbs, dragging their hindquarters in a similar manner to that used by the <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">elephant seal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ichthyostega_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ichthyostega-21">[21]</a></sup> In the early <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> (360 to 345 million years ago), the climate became wet and warm. Extensive swamps developed with <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsetail" title="Horsetail">horsetails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calamites" title="Calamites">calamites</a>. Air-breathing <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> evolved and invaded the land where they provided food for the <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivorous</a> amphibians that began to adapt to the terrestrial environment. There were no other tetrapods on the land and the amphibians were at the top of the food chain, occupying the ecological position currently held by the crocodile. Though equipped with limbs and the ability to breathe air, most still had a long tapering body and strong tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoczynska_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spoczynska-23">[23]</a></sup> They were the top land predators, sometimes reaching several metres in length, preying on the large insects of the period and the many types of fish in the water. They still needed to return to water to lay their shell-less eggs, and even most modern amphibians have a fully aquatic larval stage with gills like their fish ancestors. It was the development of the <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotic</a> egg, which prevents the developing embryo from drying out, that enabled the reptiles to reproduce on land and which led to their <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ecology)" title="Dominance (ecology)">dominance</a> in the period that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evoamphib_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-evoamphib-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous_rainforest_collapse" title="Carboniferous rainforest collapse">Carboniferous rainforest collapse</a> amphibian dominance gave way to reptiles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates-24">[24]</a></sup> and amphibians were further devastated by the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-25">[25]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic Period</a> (250 to 200 million years ago), the reptiles continued to out-compete the amphibians, leading to a reduction in both the amphibians' size and their importance in the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. According to the fossil record, <a href="/wiki/Lissamphibia" title="Lissamphibia">Lissamphibia</a>, which includes all modern amphibians and is the only surviving lineage, may have branched off from the extinct groups <a href="/wiki/Temnospondyli" title="Temnospondyli">Temnospondyli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepospondyli" title="Lepospondyli">Lepospondyli</a> at some period between the Late Carboniferous and the Early Triassic. The relative scarcity of fossil evidence precludes precise dating,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll-20">[20]</a></sup> but the most recent molecular study, based on <a href="/wiki/Multilocus_sequence_typing" title="Multilocus sequence typing">multilocus sequence typing</a>, suggests a Late Carboniferous/<a href="/wiki/Cisuralian" title="Cisuralian">Early Permian</a> origin for extant amphibians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG"><img alt="Eryops" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1475" data-file-width="3190" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG/220px-Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG/330px-Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG/440px-Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eryops_-_National_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_IMG_1974.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The temnospondyl <i><a href="/wiki/Eryops" title="Eryops">Eryops</a></i> had sturdy limbs to support its body on land</div></div></div>
<p>The origins and evolutionary relationships between the three main groups of amphibians is a matter of debate. A 2005 molecular phylogeny, based on <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_DNA" title="Ribosomal DNA">rDNA</a> analysis, suggests that salamanders and caecilians are more closely related to each other than they are to frogs. It also appears that the divergence of the three groups took place in the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> or early <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> (around 250 million years ago), before the breakup of the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> and soon after their divergence from the lobe-finned fish. The briefness of this period, and the swiftness with which radiation took place, would help account for the relative scarcity of primitive amphibian fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> There are large gaps in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a>, the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Dissorophoidea" title="Dissorophoidea">dissorophoid</a> temnospondyl <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerobatrachus_hottoni" title="Gerobatrachus hottoni"><i>Gerobatrachus</i></a> from the Early Permian in Texas in 2008 provided a missing link with many of the characteristics of modern frogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">Molecular analysis</a> suggests that the frog–salamander divergence took place considerably earlier than the <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">palaeontological</a> evidence indicates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-14">[14]</a></sup> One study suggested suggested that the last common ancestor of all modern amphibians lived about 315 million years ago, and that <a href="/wiki/Stereospondyli" title="Stereospondyli">stereospondyl</a> temnospondyls are the closest relatives to the caecilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> However, most studies support a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> origin of all modern amphibians within the dissorophoid temnospondyls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>As they evolved from lunged fish, amphibians had to make certain adaptations for living on land, including the need to develop new means of locomotion. In the water, the sideways thrusts of their tails had propelled them forward, but on land, quite different mechanisms were required. Their vertebral columns, limbs, limb girdles and musculature needed to be strong enough to raise them off the ground for locomotion and feeding. Terrestrial adults discarded their <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral line</a> systems and adapted their sensory systems to receive stimuli via the medium of the air. They needed to develop new methods to regulate their body heat to cope with fluctuations in ambient temperature. They developed behaviours suitable for reproduction in a terrestrial environment. Their skins were exposed to harmful <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> rays that had previously been absorbed by the water. The skin changed to become more protective and prevent excessive water loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<p>The superclass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrapoda" title="Tetrapoda">Tetrapoda</a> is divided into four classes of vertebrate animals with four limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Reptiles, birds and mammals are amniotes, the eggs of which are either laid or carried by the female and are surrounded by several membranes, some of which are impervious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Lacking these membranes, amphibians require water bodies for reproduction, although some species have developed various strategies for protecting or bypassing the vulnerable aquatic larval stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859-29">[29]</a></sup> They are not found in the sea with the exception of one or two frogs that live in <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish water</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> swamps;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Anderson%27s_salamander" title="Anderson's salamander">Anderson's salamander</a> meanwhile occurs in brackish or salt water lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> On land, amphibians are restricted to moist habitats because of the need to keep their skin damp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern amphibians have a simplified anatomy compared to their ancestors due to <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">paedomorphosis</a>, caused by two evolutionary trends: miniaturization and an unusually large genome, which result in a slower growth and development rate compared to other vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Another reason for their size is associated with their rapid metamorphosis, which seems to have evolved only in the ancestors of lissamphibia; in all other known lines the development was much more gradual. Because a remodeling of the feeding apparatus means they don't eat during the metamorphosis, the metamorphosis has to go faster the smaller the individual is, so it happens at an early stage when the larvae are still small. (The largest species of salamanders don't go through a metamorphosis.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Amphibians that lay eggs on land often go through the whole metamorphosis inside the egg. An anamniotic terrestrial egg is less than 1 cm in diameter due to diffusion problems, a size which puts a limit on the amount of posthatching growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The smallest amphibian (and vertebrate) in the world is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microhylid" title="Microhylid">microhylid</a> frog from <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis" title="Paedophryne amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></i>) first discovered in 2012. It has an average length of 7.7 mm (0.30 in) and is part of a genus that contains four of the world's ten smallest frog species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The largest living amphibian is the 1.8 m (5 ft 11 in) <a href="/wiki/Chinese_giant_salamander" title="Chinese giant salamander">Chinese giant salamander</a> (<i>Andrias davidianus</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amphibianfacts_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amphibianfacts-39">[39]</a></sup> but this is a great deal smaller than the largest amphibian that ever existed—the extinct 9 m (30 ft) <i><a href="/wiki/Prionosuchus" title="Prionosuchus">Prionosuchus</a></i>, a crocodile-like temnospondyl dating to 270 million years ago from the middle Permian of Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The largest frog is the African <a href="/wiki/Goliath_frog" title="Goliath frog">Goliath frog</a> (<i>Conraua goliath</i>), which can reach 32 cm (13 in) and weigh 3 kg (6.6 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amphibianfacts_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amphibianfacts-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amphibians are ectothermic (cold-blooded) vertebrates that do not maintain their body temperature through internal <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> processes. Their <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a> is low and as a result, their food and energy requirements are limited. In the adult state, they have tear ducts and movable eyelids, and most species have ears that can detect airborne or ground vibrations. They have muscular tongues, which in many species can be protruded. Modern amphibians have fully <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossified</a> vertebrae with <a href="/wiki/Articular_processes" title="Articular processes">articular processes</a>. Their ribs are usually short and may be fused to the vertebrae. Their skulls are mostly broad and short, and are often incompletely ossified. Their skin contains little <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> and lacks scales, apart from a few fish-like scales in certain caecilians. The skin contains many <a href="/wiki/Mucous_gland" title="Mucous gland">mucous glands</a> and in some species, <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">poison glands</a> (a type of granular gland). The hearts of amphibians have three chambers, two <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atria</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a>. They have a <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">urinary bladder</a> and nitrogenous waste products are excreted primarily as <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>. Most amphibians lay their eggs in water and have aquatic larvae that undergo metamorphosis to become terrestrial adults. Amphibians breathe by means of a pump action in which air is first drawn into the <a href="/wiki/Buccopharyngeal_membrane" title="Buccopharyngeal membrane">buccopharyngeal</a> region through the nostrils. These are then closed and the air is forced into the lungs by contraction of the throat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199524–25_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199524–25-41">[41]</a></sup> They supplement this with <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> through the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991843–859-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anura">Anura</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg"><img alt="Red-eyed tree frog" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1720" data-file-width="2293" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/220px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/330px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/440px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Agalychnis_callidryas" title="Agalychnis callidryas">Red-eyed tree frog</a> (<i>Agalychnis callidryas</i>) with limbs and feet specialised for climbing</div></div></div>
<p>The order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anura_(frog)" title="Anura (frog)">Anura</a> (from the Ancient Greek <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privative_a" title="Privative a">a(n)-</a></i> meaning "without" and <i>oura</i> meaning "tail") comprises the frogs and toads. They usually have long hind limbs that fold underneath them, shorter forelimbs, webbed toes with no claws, no tails, large eyes and glandular moist skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953-13">[13]</a></sup> Members of this order with smooth skins are commonly referred to as frogs, while those with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/warty" title="wikt:warty">warty</a> skins are known as toads. The difference is not a formal one taxonomically and there are numerous exceptions to this rule. Members of the family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bufonidae" title="Bufonidae">Bufonidae</a> are known as the "true toads".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Frogs range in size from the 30-centimetre (12 in) <a href="/wiki/Goliath_frog" title="Goliath frog">Goliath frog</a> (<i>Conraua goliath</i>) of West Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> to the 7.7-millimetre (0.30 in) <i><a href="/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis" title="Paedophryne amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></i>, first described in Papua New Guinea in 2012, which is also the smallest known vertebrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Although most species are associated with water and damp habitats, some are specialised to live in trees or in deserts. They are found worldwide except for polar areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anura is divided into three suborders that are broadly accepted by the scientific community, but the relationships between some families remain unclear. Future <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular</a> studies should provide further insights into their evolutionary relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The suborder <a href="/wiki/Archaeobatrachia" title="Archaeobatrachia">Archaeobatrachia</a> contains four families of primitive frogs. These are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascaphidae" title="Ascaphidae">Ascaphidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombinatoridae" title="Bombinatoridae">Bombinatoridae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discoglossidae" title="Discoglossidae">Discoglossidae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leiopelmatidae" title="Leiopelmatidae">Leiopelmatidae</a> which have few derived features and are probably paraphyletic with regard to other frog lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-47">[47]</a></sup> The six families in the more evolutionarily advanced suborder <a href="/wiki/Mesobatrachia" title="Mesobatrachia">Mesobatrachia</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Fossorial" title="Fossorial">fossorial</a> <a href="/wiki/Megophryidae" title="Megophryidae">Megophryidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelobatidae" title="Pelobatidae">Pelobatidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pelodytidae" title="Pelodytidae">Pelodytidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scaphiopodidae" title="Scaphiopodidae">Scaphiopodidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhinophrynidae" title="Rhinophrynidae">Rhinophrynidae</a> and the obligatorily aquatic <a href="/wiki/Pipidae" title="Pipidae">Pipidae</a>. These have certain characteristics that are intermediate between the two other suborders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neobatrachia" title="Neobatrachia">Neobatrachia</a> is by far the largest suborder and includes the remaining families of modern frogs, including most common species. Ninety-six percent of the over 5,000 extant species of frog are neobatrachians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caudata">Caudata</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg"><img alt="Japanese giant salamander" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="433" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg/170px-Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg/255px-Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg/340px-Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cryptobranchus_japonicus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Japanese_giant_salamander" title="Japanese giant salamander">Japanese giant salamander</a> <br/>(<i>Andrias japonicus</i>), a primitive salamander</div></div></div>
<p>The order <a href="/wiki/Caudata" title="Caudata">Caudata</a> (from the Latin <i>cauda</i> meaning "tail") consists of the salamanders—elongated, low-slung animals that mostly resemble lizards in form. This is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">symplesiomorphic trait</a> and they are no more closely related to lizards than they are to mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Salamanders lack claws, have scale-free skins, either smooth or covered with <a href="/wiki/Tubercle" title="Tubercle">tubercles</a>, and tails that are usually flattened from side to side and often finned. They range in size from the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_giant_salamander" title="Chinese giant salamander">Chinese giant salamander</a> (<i>Andrias davidianus</i>), which has been reported to grow to a length of 1.8 metres (5 ft 11 in),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> to the diminutive <i><a href="/wiki/Thorius_pennatulus" title="Thorius pennatulus">Thorius pennatulus</a></i> from Mexico which seldom exceeds 20 mm (0.8 in) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Salamanders have a mostly <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasian</a> distribution, being present in much of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holarctic" title="Holarctic">Holarctic</a> region of the northern hemisphere. The family <a href="/wiki/Plethodontidae" title="Plethodontidae">Plethodontidae</a> is also found in Central America and South America north of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-45">[45]</a></sup> South America was apparently invaded from Central America by about the start of the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, 23 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elmer2013_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elmer2013-52">[52]</a></sup> Urodela is a name sometimes used for all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species of salamanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson-53">[53]</a></sup> Members of several salamander families have become <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">paedomorphic</a> and either fail to complete their metamorphosis or retain some larval characteristics as adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991852_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991852-54">[54]</a></sup> Most salamanders are under 15 cm (6 in) long. They may be terrestrial or aquatic and many spend part of the year in each habitat. When on land, they mostly spend the day hidden under stones or logs or in dense vegetation, emerging in the evening and night to forage for worms, insects and other invertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg"><img alt="Danube crested newt" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="309" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg/220px-Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg/330px-Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg/440px-Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triturus_dobrogicus_dunai_tarajosg%C5%91te.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Danube_crested_newt" title="Danube crested newt">Danube crested newt</a> <br/>(<i>Triturus dobrogicus</i>), an advanced salamander</div></div></div>
<p>The suborder <a href="/wiki/Cryptobranchoidea" title="Cryptobranchoidea">Cryptobranchoidea</a> contains the primitive salamanders. A number of fossil cryptobranchids have been found, but there are only three living species, the Chinese giant salamander (<i>Andrias davidianus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_giant_salamander" title="Japanese giant salamander">Japanese giant salamander</a> (<i>Andrias japonicus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Hellbender" title="Hellbender">hellbender</a> (<i>Cryptobranchus alleganiensis</i>) from North America. These large amphibians retain several larval characteristics in their adult state; gills slits are present and the eyes are unlidded. A unique feature is their ability to feed by suction, depressing either the left side of their lower jaw or the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADWCrypto_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADWCrypto-55">[55]</a></sup> The males excavate nests, persuade females to lay their egg strings inside them, and guard them. As well as breathing with lungs, they respire through the many folds in their thin skin, which has <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillaries</a> close to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHSAR_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-EHSAR-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The suborder <a href="/wiki/Salamandroidea" title="Salamandroidea">Salamandroidea</a> contains the advanced salamanders. They differ from the cryptobranchids by having fused <a href="/wiki/Mandible" title="Mandible">prearticular bones</a> in the lower jaw, and by using internal fertilisation. In salamandrids, the male deposits a bundle of sperm, the <a href="/wiki/Spermatophore" title="Spermatophore">spermatophore</a>, and the female picks it up and inserts it into her cloaca where the sperm is stored until the eggs are laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritCaudata_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritCaudata-57">[57]</a></sup> The largest family in this group is Plethodontidae, the lungless salamanders, which includes 60% of all salamander species. The <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Salamandridae" title="Salamandridae">Salamandridae</a> includes the true salamanders and the name "<a href="/wiki/Newt" title="Newt">newt</a>" is given to members of its subfamily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleurodelinae" title="Pleurodelinae">Pleurodelinae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19953-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The third suborder, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirenoidea" title="Sirenoidea">Sirenoidea</a>, contains the four species of sirens, which are in a single family, <a href="/wiki/Sirenidae" title="Sirenidae">Sirenidae</a>. Members of this order are <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eel</a>-like aquatic salamanders with much reduced forelimbs and no hind limbs. Some of their features are primitive while others are derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Fertilisation is likely to be external as sirenids lack the cloacal glands used by male salamandrids to produce spermatophores and the females lack <a href="/wiki/Spermatheca" title="Spermatheca">spermathecae</a> for sperm storage. Despite this, the eggs are laid singly, a behaviour not conducive for external fertilisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritCaudata_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritCaudata-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gymnophiona">Gymnophiona</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg"><img alt="Siphonops paulensis" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="788" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg/220px-Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg/330px-Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg/440px-Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siphonops_paulensis02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The limbless South American caecilian <i><a href="/wiki/Siphonops_paulensis" title="Siphonops paulensis">Siphonops paulensis</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnophiona" title="Gymnophiona">Gymnophiona</a> (from the Greek <i>gymnos</i> meaning "naked" and <i>ophis</i> meaning "serpent") or Apoda comprises the caecilians. These are long, cylindrical, limbless animals with a snake- or <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">worm-like</a> form. The adults vary in length from 8 to 75 centimetres (3 to 30 inches) with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Caecilia_thompsoni" title="Caecilia thompsoni">Thomson's caecilian</a> (<i>Caecilia thompsoni</i>), which can reach 150 centimetres (4.9 feet). A caecilian's skin has a large number of transverse folds and in some species contains tiny embedded dermal scales. It has rudimentary eyes covered in skin, which are probably limited to discerning differences in light intensity. It also has a pair of short <a href="/wiki/Tentacle" title="Tentacle">tentacles</a> near the eye that can be extended and which have <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">tactile</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfactory</a> functions. Most caecilians live underground in burrows in damp soil, in rotten wood and under plant debris, but some are aquatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19954_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19954-59">[59]</a></sup> Most species lay their eggs underground and when the larvae hatch, they make their way to adjacent bodies of water. Others brood their eggs and the larvae undergo metamorphosis before the eggs hatch. A few species give birth to live young, nourishing them with glandular secretions while they are in the oviduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991858_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991858-60">[60]</a></sup> Caecilians have a mostly <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwanan</a> distribution, being found in tropical regions of Africa, Asia and Central and South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin">Skin</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_frog_congo.jpg"><img alt="Common reed frog" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tree_frog_congo.jpg/170px-Tree_frog_congo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tree_frog_congo.jpg/255px-Tree_frog_congo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Tree_frog_congo.jpg/340px-Tree_frog_congo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_frog_congo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">bright colours</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Common_reed_frog" title="Common reed frog">common reed frog</a> (<i>Hyperolius viridiflavus</i>) are typical of a toxic species</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/integument" title="wikt:integument">integumentary</a> structure contains some typical characteristics common to terrestrial vertebrates, such as the presence of highly <a href="/wiki/Keratin#Cornification" title="Keratin">cornified</a> outer layers, renewed periodically through a moulting process controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">pituitary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> glands. Local thickenings (often called warts) are common, such as those found on toads. The outside of the skin is shed periodically mostly in one piece, in contrast to mammals and birds where it is shed in flakes. Amphibians often eat the sloughed skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-45">[45]</a></sup> Caecilians are unique among amphibians in having mineralized dermal scales embedded in the <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a> between the furrows in the skin. The similarity of these to the scales of bony fish is largely superficial. <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">Lizards</a> and some frogs have somewhat similar <a href="/wiki/Osteoderm" title="Osteoderm">osteoderms</a> forming bony deposits in the dermis, but this is an example of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> with similar structures having arisen independently in diverse vertebrate lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FrogSkin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="757" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/FrogSkin.png/300px-FrogSkin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/FrogSkin.png/450px-FrogSkin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/FrogSkin.png/600px-FrogSkin.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FrogSkin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross section of frog skin. <div><ol style="list-style-type:upper-alpha"><li>Mucous gland</li><li>Chromatophore</li><li>Granular poison gland</li><li><a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">Connective tissue</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Stratum_corneum" title="Stratum corneum">Stratum corneum</a></li><li>Transition zone</li><li><a href="/wiki/Epidermis" title="Epidermis">Epidermis</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">Dermis</a></li></ol></div></div></div></div>
<p>Amphibian skin is permeable to water. Gas exchange can take place through the skin (<a href="/wiki/Cutaneous_respiration" title="Cutaneous respiration">cutaneous respiration</a>) and this allows adult amphibians to respire without rising to the surface of water and to hibernate at the bottom of ponds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-45">[45]</a></sup> To compensate for their thin and delicate skin, amphibians have evolved mucous glands, principally on their heads, backs and tails. The secretions produced by these help keep the skin moist. In addition, most species of amphibian have granular glands that secrete distasteful or poisonous substances. Some amphibian toxins can be lethal to humans while others have little effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amphibfacts_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-amphibfacts-63">[63]</a></sup> The main poison-producing glands, the <a href="/wiki/Parotoid_gland" title="Parotoid gland">parotoids</a>, produce the neurotoxin <a href="/wiki/Bufotoxin" title="Bufotoxin">bufotoxin</a> and are located behind the ears of toads, along the backs of frogs, behind the eyes of salamanders and on the upper surface of caecilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199510–11_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199510–11-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The skin colour of amphibians is produced by three layers of pigment cells called <a href="/wiki/Chromatophore" title="Chromatophore">chromatophores</a>. These three cell layers consist of the melanophores (occupying the deepest layer), the guanophores (forming an intermediate layer and containing many granules, producing a blue-green colour) and the lipophores (yellow, the most superficial layer). The colour change displayed by many species is initiated by <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> secreted by the pituitary gland. Unlike bony fish, there is no direct control of the pigment cells by the nervous system, and this results in the colour change taking place more slowly than happens in fish. A vividly coloured skin usually indicates that the species is toxic and is a warning sign to predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skeletal_system_and_locomotion">Skeletal system and locomotion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="775" data-file-width="875" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg/220px-Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg/330px-Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg/440px-Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenotosuchus_skull_roof.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skull diagram of <i><a href="/wiki/Xenotosuchus" title="Xenotosuchus">Xenotosuchus</a>,</i> a temnospondyl</div></div></div>
<p>Amphibians have a skeletal system that is structurally <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> to other tetrapods, though with a number of variations. They all have four limbs except for the legless caecilians and a few species of salamander with reduced or no limbs. The bones are hollow and lightweight. The musculoskeletal system is strong to enable it to support the head and body. The bones are fully <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossified</a> and the vertebrae interlock with each other by means of overlapping processes. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectoral_girdle" title="Pectoral girdle">pectoral girdle</a> is supported by muscle, and the well-developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelvic_girdle" title="Pelvic girdle">pelvic girdle</a> is attached to the backbone by a pair of sacral ribs. The <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> slopes forward and the body is held closer to the ground than is the case in mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg"><img alt="Frog skeleton" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/220px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/330px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/440px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of the <a href="/wiki/Surinam_horned_frog" title="Surinam horned frog">Surinam horned frog</a><br/>(<i>Ceratophrys cornuta</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>In most amphibians, there are four digits on the fore foot and five on the hind foot, but no claws on either. Some salamanders have fewer digits and the <a href="/wiki/Amphiuma" title="Amphiuma">amphiumas</a> are eel-like in appearance with tiny, stubby legs. The <a href="/wiki/Siren_(genus)" title="Siren (genus)">sirens</a> are aquatic salamanders with stumpy forelimbs and no hind limbs. The caecilians are limbless. They burrow in the manner of earthworms with zones of muscle contractions moving along the body. On the surface of the ground or in water they move by undulating their body from side to side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In frogs, the hind legs are larger than the fore legs, especially so in those species that principally move by jumping or swimming. In the walkers and runners the hind limbs are not so large, and the burrowers mostly have short limbs and broad bodies. The feet have adaptations for the way of life, with webbing between the toes for swimming, broad adhesive toe pads for climbing, and keratinised tubercles on the hind feet for digging (frogs usually dig backwards into the soil). In most salamanders, the limbs are short and more or less the same length and project at right angles from the body. Locomotion on land is by walking and the tail often swings from side to side or is used as a prop, particularly when climbing. In their normal gait, only one leg is advanced at a time in the manner adopted by their ancestors, the lobe-finned fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846-66">[66]</a></sup> Some salamanders in the genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aneides" title="Aneides">Aneides</a></i> and certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungless_salamander" title="Lungless salamander">plethodontids</a> climb trees and have long limbs, large toepads and prehensile tails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritCaudata_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritCaudata-57">[57]</a></sup> In aquatic salamanders and in frog tadpoles, the tail has <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral_fin" title="Ventral fin">ventral</a> fins and is moved from side to side as a means of propulsion. Adult frogs do not have tails and caecilians have only very short ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/140px-Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/210px-Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/280px-Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Didactic_model_of_an_amphibian_heart-FMVZ_USP-14.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Educational_toy" title="Educational toy">Didactic model</a> of an amphibian heart.</div></div></div>
<p>Salamanders use their tails in defence and some are prepared to jettison them to save their lives in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Autotomy" title="Autotomy">autotomy</a>. Certain species in the Plethodontidae have a weak zone at the base of the tail and use this strategy readily. The tail often continues to twitch after separation which may distract the attacker and allow the salamander to escape. Both tails and limbs can be regenerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beneski_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beneski-68">[68]</a></sup> Adult frogs are unable to regrow limbs but tadpoles can do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199526–36-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="234" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg/220px-Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg/330px-Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg/440px-Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juvenile_Amphibian_Circulatory_System.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile amphibian circulatory systems are single loop systems which resemble fish. <div><ol><li>Internal gills where the blood is reoxygenated</li><li>Point where the blood is depleted of oxygen and returns to the heart via veins</li><li>Two chambered heart</li></ol></div> Red indicates oxygenated blood, and blue represents oxygen depleted blood.</div></div></div>
<p>Amphibians have a juvenile stage and an adult stage, and the circulatory systems of the two are distinct. In the juvenile (or tadpole) stage, the circulation is similar to that of a fish; the two-chambered heart pumps the blood through the gills where it is oxygenated, and is spread around the body and back to the heart in a single loop. In the adult stage, amphibians (especially frogs) lose their gills and develop lungs. They have a heart that consists of a single ventricle and two atria. When the ventricle starts contracting, deoxygenated blood is pumped through the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> to the lungs. Continued contraction then pumps oxygenated blood around the rest of the body. Mixing of the two bloodstreams is minimized by the anatomy of the chambers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991306_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991306-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_and_sensory_systems">Nervous and sensory systems</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pain_in_amphibians" title="Pain in amphibians">Pain in amphibians</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> is basically the same as in other vertebrates, with a central brain, a spinal cord, and nerves throughout the body. The amphibian brain is relatively simple but broadly the same structurally as in reptiles, birds and mammals. Their brains are elongated, except in caecilians, and contain the usual motor and sensory areas of tetrapods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pineal_body" title="Pineal body">pineal body</a>, known to regulate sleep patterns in humans, is thought to produce the hormones involved in <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aestivation" title="Aestivation">aestivation</a> in amphibians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995100_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995100-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tadpoles retain the lateral line system of their ancestral fishes, but this is lost in terrestrial adult amphibians. Some caecilians possess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroreception" title="Electroreception">electroreceptors</a> that allow them to locate objects around them when submerged in water. The ears are well developed in frogs. There is no external ear, but the large circular <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(anatomy)" title="Tympanum (anatomy)">eardrum</a> lies on the surface of the head just behind the eye. This vibrates and sound is transmitted through a single bone, the <a href="/wiki/Stapes" title="Stapes">stapes</a>, to the inner ear. Only high-frequency sounds like mating calls are heard in this way, but low-frequency noises can be detected through another mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846-66">[66]</a></sup> There is a patch of specialized haircells, called <i>papilla amphibiorum</i>, in the inner ear capable of detecting deeper sounds. Another feature, unique to frogs and salamanders, is the columella-operculum complex adjoining the auditory capsule which is involved in the transmission of both airborne and seismic signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199569_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199569-72">[72]</a></sup> The ears of salamanders and caecilians are less highly developed than those of frogs as they do not normally communicate with each other through the medium of sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBAmphibia_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBAmphibia-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The eyes of tadpoles lack lids, but at metamorphosis, the <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a> becomes more dome-shaped, the <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a> becomes flatter, and <a href="/wiki/Eyelid" title="Eyelid">eyelids</a> and associated glands and ducts develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991846-66">[66]</a></sup> The adult eyes are an improvement on invertebrate eyes and were a first step in the development of more advanced vertebrate eyes. They allow colour vision and depth of focus. In the retinas are green rods, which are receptive to a wide range of wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBAmphibia_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-EBAmphibia-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestive_and_excretory_systems">Digestive and excretory systems</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG"><img alt="Dissected frog" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1668" data-file-width="1248" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/220px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/330px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/440px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dissected frog: <div><ol><li>Right atrium</li><li>Liver</li><li>Aorta</li><li>Egg mass</li><li>Colon</li><li>Left atrium</li><li>Ventricle</li><li>Stomach</li><li>Left lung</li><li>Spleen</li><li>Small intestine</li><li>Cloaca</li></ol></div></div></div></div>
<p>Many amphibians catch their prey by flicking out an elongated tongue with a sticky tip and drawing it back into the mouth before seizing the item with their jaws.
